<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2009 457:7229"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2009 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sorghum, an African grass related to sugar cane and maize, is grown for food, feed, fibre and fuel. We present an initial analysis of the &#8764;730-megabase Sorghum bicolor (L.) Moench genome, placing &#8764;98% of genes in their chromosomal context using whole-genome shotgun sequence validated by genetic, physical and syntenic information. Genetic recombination is largely confined to about one-third of the sorghum genome with gene order and density similar to those of rice. Retrotransposon accumulation in recombinationally recalcitrant heterochromatin explains the &#8764;75% larger genome size of sorghum compared with rice. Although gene and repetitive DNA distributions have been preserved since palaeopolyploidization &#8764;70 million years ago, most duplicated gene sets lost one member before the sorghum&#8211;rice divergence. Concerted evolution makes one duplicated chromosomal segment appear to be only a few million years old. About 24% of genes are grass-specific and 7% are sorghum-specific. Recent gene and microRNA duplications may contribute to sorghum&#8217;s drought tolerance. The Sorghum bicolor genome sequence is published this week. Sorghum is a cereal grown widely as food, animal feed, fibre and fuel. Tolerant to hot, dry conditions, it is a staple for large populations in the West African Sahel region. Comparisons of the genome with those of maize and rice shed light on the evolution of grasses and of C4 photosynthesis, which is particularly efficient at assimilating carbon at high temperatures. In addition, protein coding genes and miRNAs that could contribute to sorghum&#39;s drought tolerance may also be found. Sorghum yield improvement has lagged behind that of other crops and the availability of the genome sequence could provide a vital boost to work on its improvement. Sorghum is an African grass that is grown for food, animal feed and fuel. The current paper presents an initial analysis of the &#8764;730 megabase genome of Sorghum bicolor. Genome analysis and its comparison with maize and rice shed light on grass genome evolution and also provide insights into the evolution of C4 photosynthesis, as well as protein coding genes and miRNAs that might contribute to sorghum&#39;s drought tolerance."/>

        <meta property="og:title" content="The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Sorghum is an African grass that is grown for food, animal feed and fuel. The current paper presents an initial analysis of the â¼730 megabase genome of Sorghum bicolor. Genome analysis and its comparison with maize and rice shed light on grass genome evolution and also provide insights into the evolution of C4 photosynthesis, as well as protein coding genes and miRNAs that might contribute to sorghum&#x27;s drought tolerance."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sorghum, an African grass related to sugar cane and maize, is grown for food, feed, fibre and fuel. We present an initial analysis of the <span class="stix">â¼</span>730-megabase <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> (L.) Moench genome, placing <span class="stix">â¼</span>98% of genes in their chromosomal context using whole-genome shotgun sequence validated by genetic, physical and syntenic information. Genetic recombination is largely confined to about one-third of the sorghum genome with gene order and density similar to those of rice. Retrotransposon accumulation in recombinationally recalcitrant heterochromatin explains the <span class="stix">â¼</span>75% larger genome size of sorghum compared with rice. Although gene and repetitive DNA distributions have been preserved since palaeopolyploidization <span class="stix">â¼</span>70 million years ago, most duplicated gene sets lost one member before the sorghumârice divergence. Concerted evolution makes one duplicated chromosomal segment appear to be only a few million years old. About 24% of genes are grass-specific and 7% are sorghum-specific. Recent gene and microRNA duplications may contribute to sorghumâs drought tolerance.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Saccharinae plants include some of the most efficient biomass accumulators, providing food and fuel from starch (sorghum) and sugar (sorghum and <i>Saccharum</i>, sugar cane), and have potential for use as cellulosic biofuel crops (sorghum, sugar cane, <i>Miscanthus</i>). Of singular importance to Saccharinae productivity is C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis, comprising biochemical and morphological specializations that increase net carbon assimilation at high temperatures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Hatch, M. D. &amp; Slack, C. R. Photosynthesis by sugar-cane leavesâa new carboxylation reaction and pathway of sugar formation. Biochem. J. 101, 103 (1966)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1183">1</a></sup>. Despite their common photosynthetic strategy, the Saccharinae show much morphological and genomic variation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><p>Its small genome (<span class="stix">â¼</span>730âMb) makes sorghum an attractive model for functional genomics of Saccharinae and other C<sub>4</sub> grasses. Rice, the first fully sequenced cereal genome, is more representative of C<sub>3</sub> photosynthetic grasses. Drought tolerance makes sorghum especially important in dry regions such as northeast Africa (its centre of diversity) and the southern plains of the United States. Genetic variation in the partitioning of carbon into sugar stores versus cell wall mass, and in perenniality and associated features such as tillering and stalk reserve retention<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The weediness of wild plantsâmolecular analysis of genes influencing dispersal and persistence of johnsongrass, Sorghum halepense (l) pers. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 92, 6127â6131 (1995)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1197">2</a></sup>, make sorghum an attractive system for the study of traits important in perennial cellulosic biomass crops. Its high level of inbreeding makes it an attractive association genetics system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hamblin, M. T. et al. Equilibrium processes cannot explain high levels of short- and medium-range linkage disequilibrium in the domesticated grass Sorghum bicolour . Genetics 171, 1247â1256 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1201">3</a></sup>. Transgenic approaches to sorghum improvement are constrained by high gene flow to weedy relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Morrell, P. L. et al. Crop-to-weed introgression has impacted allelic composition of johnsongrass populations with and without recent exposure to cultivated sorghum. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2143â2154 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1205">4</a></sup>, making knowledge of its intrinsic genetic potential all the more important.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Reconstructing a repeat-rich genome from shotgun sequences"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Reconstructing a repeat-rich genome from shotgun sequences</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>Preferred approaches to sequencing entire genomes are currently to apply shotgun sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gardner, R. C. et al. The complete nucleotide sequence of an infectious clone of cauliflower mosaic virus by M13mp7 shotgun sequencing. Nucleic Acids Res. 9, 2871â2888 (1981)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1217">5</a></sup> either to a minimum âtiling pathâ of genomic clones, or to genomic DNA directly. The latter approach, whole-genome shotgun (WGS) sequencing, is widely used for mammalian genomes, being fast, relatively economical and reducing cloning bias. However, its applicability has been questioned for repetitive DNA-rich plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1221">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Despite a repeat content of <span class="stix">â¼</span>61%, a high-quality genome sequence was assembled from homozygous sorghum genotype BTx623 by using WGS and incorporating the following: (1) <span class="stix">â¼</span>8.5 genome equivalents of paired-end reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Vieira, J. &amp; Messing, J. The pUC plasmids, an M13mp7-derived system for insertion mutagenesis and sequencing with synthetic universal primers. Gene 19, 259â268 (1982)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1228">7</a></sup> from genomic libraries spanning a <span class="stix">â¼</span>100-fold range of insert sizes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Table 1</a>), resolving many repetitive regions; and (2) high-quality read length averaging 723âbp, facilitating assembly. Comparison with 27 finished bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) showed the WGS assembly to beâ&gt;98.46% complete and accurate toâ&lt;1 error per 10âkb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 2.5</a>).</p><p>Comparison with a high-density genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. A high-density genetic recombination map of sequence-tagged sites for Sorghum, as a framework for comparative structural and evolutionary genomics of tropical grains and grasses. Genetics 165, 367â386 (2003)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1241">8</a></sup>, a âfinger-print contigâ (FPC)-based physical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206â13211 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1245">9</a></sup>, and the rice sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1249">6</a></sup> improved the sorghum WGS assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Notes 1 and 2</a>). Among the 201 largest scaffolds (spanning 678.9âMb, 97.3% of the assembly), 28 showed discrepancies with two or more of these lines of evidence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 2.6</a>), often near repetitive elements. After breaking the assembly at the points of discrepancy, the resulting 229 scaffolds have an N50 (number of scaffolds that collectively cover at least 50% of the assembly) of 35 and L50 (length of the shortest scaffold among those that collectively cover 50% of the assembly) of 7.0âMb. A total of 38 (2%) of 1,869 FPC contigs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206â13211 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1260">9</a></sup> were deemed erroneous, containingâ&gt;5 BAC ends that fell into different sequence scaffolds.</p><p>A total of 127 scaffolds containing 625.7âMb (89.7%) of DNA and 1,476 FPC contigs could be assigned to chromosomal locations and oriented. Fifteen out of twenty chromosome ends terminated in telomeric repeats. The other 102 scaffolds were generally smaller (53.2âMb, 7.6%), with 85 (83%) containing far greater-than-average abundance of the Cen38 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Miller, J. T. et al. Cloning and characterization of a centromere-specific repetitive DNA element from Sorghum bicolour . Theor. Appl. Genet. 96, 832â839 (1998)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1266">10</a>) centromeric repeat, and with only 374 predicted genes. These 102 scaffolds merged only 193 FPC contigs, presumably due to the greater abundance of repeats that are recalcitrant to clone-based physical mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206â13211 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1270">9</a></sup> and may be omitted in BAC-by-BAC approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Venter, J. C. et al. Shotgun sequencing of the human genome. Science 280, 1540â1542 (1998)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1274">11</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Genome size evolution and its causes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Genome size evolution and its causes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>The <span class="stix">â¼</span>75% larger quantity of DNA in the genome of sorghum compared with rice is mostly heterochromatin. Alignment to genetic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. A high-density genetic recombination map of sequence-tagged sites for Sorghum, as a framework for comparative structural and evolutionary genomics of tropical grains and grasses. Genetics 165, 367â386 (2003)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1286">8</a></sup> and cytological maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kim, J. S. et al. Chromosome identification and nomenclature of Sorghum bicolour . Genetics 169, 1169â1173 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1290">12</a></sup> suggests that sorghum and rice have similar quantities of euchromatin (252 and 309âMb, respectively; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Table 7</a>), accounting for 97â98% of recombination (1,025.2âcM and 1,496.5âcM, respectively) and 75.4â94.2% of genes in the respective cereals, with largely collinear gene order<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206â13211 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1297">9</a></sup>. In contrast, sorghum heterochromatin occupies at least 460âMb (62%), far more than in rice (63âMb, 15%). The <span class="stix">â¼</span>3Ã genome expansion in maize since its divergence from sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Swigonova, Z. et al. Close split of sorghum and maize genome progenitors. Genome Res. 14, 1916â1923 (2004)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1301">13</a></sup> has been more dispersedârecombinogenic DNA has grown 4.5Ã to <span class="stix">â¼</span>1,382âMb, much more than can be explained by genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Swigonova, Z. et al. On the tetraploid origin of the maize genome. Comp. Funct. Genomics 5, 281â284 (2004)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1306">14</a></sup>.</p><p>The net size expansion of the sorghum genome relative to rice largely involved long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons. The sorghum genome contains 55% retrotransposons, intermediate between the larger maize genome (79%) and smaller rice genome (26%). Sorghum more closely resembles rice in having a higher ratio of <i>gypsy</i>-like to <i>copia</i>-like elements (3.7 to 1 and 4.9 to 1) than maize (1.6 to 1: <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><p>Although recent retroelement activity is widely distributed across the sorghum genome, turnover is rapid (as in other cereals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Swigonova, Z., Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Messing, J. Structure and evolution of the r/b chromosomal regions in rice, maize and sorghum. Genetics 169, 891â906 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1325">15</a></sup>) with pericentromeric elements persisting longer. Young LTR retrotransposon insertions (&lt;0.01âmillion years (Myr) ago) appear randomly distributed along chromosomes, suggesting that they are preferentially eliminated from gene-rich regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206â13211 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1329">9</a></sup> but accumulate in gene-poor regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>; see also <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 3.1</a>). Insertion times suggest a major wave of retrotranspositionâ&lt;1âMyr ago, after a smaller wave 1â2âMyr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="
                        Genomic landscape of sorghum chromosomes 3 and 9.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: 
                        <b>Genomic landscape of sorghum chromosomes 3 and 9.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="602"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Area charts quantify retrotransposons (55%), genes (6% exons, 8% introns), DNA transposons (7%) and centromeric repeats (2%).                         <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM282">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>CACTA-like elements, the predominant sorghum DNA transposons (4.7% of the genome), seem to relocate genes and gene fragments, as do rice âPack-MULEsâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jiang, N. et al. Pack-mule transposable elements mediate gene evolution in plants. Nature 431, 569â573 (2004)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1383">16</a></sup> and maize helitrons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Brunner, S. et al. Evolution of DNA sequence nonhomologies among maize inbreds. Plant Cell 17, 343â360 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1387">17</a></sup>. Many sorghum CACTA elements are non-autonomous deletion derivatives in which transposon genes have been replaced with non-transposon DNA including exons from one or more cellular genes as exemplified for family <i>G118</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Among 13,775 CACTA elements identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 3.4</a>), 200 encode no transposon proteins but contain at least one cellular gene fragment.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="
                        CACTA element deletion derivatives that carry gene fragments.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b>CACTA element deletion derivatives that carry gene fragments.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="573"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>CACTA family G118 has only one complete and presumably autonomous âmotherâ element. Among 18 deletion derivatives, only the terminal 500â2,500âbp are conserved, with 8 carrying gene fragments internally. One relatively homogeneous subgroup (106, 111 and 112) presumably arose recently, whereas other derivatives are unique. The locations of the hits to known rice proteins are indicated as coloured boxes. The descriptions of the foreign gene fragments are indicated underneath the boxes. HP, hypothetical protein.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM283">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In total, DNA transposons constitute 7.5% of the sorghum genome, intermediate between maize (2.7%) and rice (13.7%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements, 1.7% of the genome, are associated with genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>; see also <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 3</a>) as in other cereals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1443">6</a></sup>. Helitrons, <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.8% of the genome, nearly all lack helicase in sorghum as in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Brunner, S. et al. Evolution of DNA sequence nonhomologies among maize inbreds. Plant Cell 17, 343â360 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1447">17</a></sup>, but carry fewer gene fragments in sorghum than maize (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 3.5</a>). Organellar DNA insertion has contributed only 0.085% to the sorghum nuclear genome, far less than the 0.53% of rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 2.7</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="The gene complement of sorghum"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">The gene complement of sorghum</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>Among 34,496 sorghum gene models, we found <span class="stix">â¼</span>27,640 bona fide protein-coding genes by combining homology-based and <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction methods with expressed sequences from sorghum, maize and sugar cane (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 4</a>). Evidence for alternate splicing is found in 1,491 loci.</p><p>Another 5,197 gene models are usually shorter than the bona fide genes (oftenâ&lt;150 amino acids); have few exons (often one) and no expressed sequence tag (EST) support (compared with 85% for bona fide genes); are more diverged from rice genes; and are often found in large families with âhypotheticalâ, âuncharacterizedâ and/or retroelement-associated annotations, despite repeat masking (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 4</a>). A high concentration in pericentromeric regions where bona fide genes are scarce (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>) suggests that many of these low confidence gene models are retroelement-derived. We also identified 727 processed pseudogenes and 932 models containing domains known only from transposons.</p><p>The exon size distributions of orthologous sorghum and rice genes agree closely, and intron position and phase showâ&gt;98% concordance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 5</a>). Intron size has been conserved between sorghum and rice, although it has increased in maize owing to transpositions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Haberer, G. et al. Structure and architecture of the maize genome. Plant Physiol. 139, 1612â1624 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1487">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Most paralogues in sorghum are proximally duplicated, including 5,303 genes in 1,947 families ofââ¥2 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 4.3</a>). The longest tandem gene array is 15 cytochrome P450 genes. Other sorghum-specific tandem gene expansions include haloacid dehalogenase-like hydrolases (PF00702), FNIP repeats (PF05725), and male sterility proteins (PF03015).</p><p>We confirmed the genomic locations of 67 known sorghum microRNAs (miRNAs) and identified 82 additional miRNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 4.4</a>). Five clusters located within 500âbp of each other represent putative polycistronic miRNAs, similar to those in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Oryza</i>. Natural antisense miRNA precursors (nat-miRNAs) of family miR444 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lu, C. et al. Genome-wide analysis for discovery of rice microRNAs reveals natural antisense microRNAs (nat-miRNAs). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 4951â4956 (2008)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1509">19</a>) have been identified in three copies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Comparative gene inventories of angiosperms"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Comparative gene inventories of angiosperms</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>The number and sizes of sorghum gene families are similar to those of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice and poplar (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 4.6</a>). A total of 9,503 (58%) sorghum gene families were shared among all four species and 15,225 (93%) with at least one other species. Nearly 94% (25,875) of high-confidence sorghum genes have orthologues in rice, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and/or poplar, and together these gene complements define 11,502 ancestral angiosperm gene families represented in at least one contemporary grass and rosid genome. However, 3,983 (24%) gene families have members only in the grasses sorghum and rice; 1,153 (7%) appear to be unique to sorghum.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="
                        Orthologous gene families between sorghum, 
                        Arabidopsis
                        , rice and poplar.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: 
                        <b>Orthologous gene families between sorghum, </b>
                        <b><i>Arabidopsis</i></b>
                        <b>, rice and poplar.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="600" height="589"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The numbers of gene families (clusters) and the total numbers of clustered genes are indicated for each species and species intersection.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM284">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Pfam domains that are over-represented, under-represented or even absent in sorghum relative to rice, poplar and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, may reflect biological peculiarities specific to the <i>Sorghum</i> lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Domains over-represented in sorghum are usually present in the other organisms, a notable exception being the Î±-kafirin domain that accounts for most seed storage protein and corresponds to maize zeins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xu, J.-H. &amp; Messing, J. Organization of the prolamin gene family provides insight into the evolution of the maize genome and gene duplications in grass species. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 14330â14335 (2008)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1580">20</a></sup> but which is absent from rice.</p><p>Nucleotide-binding-siteâleucine-rich-repeat (NBS-LRR) containing proteins associated with the plant immune system are only about half as frequent in sorghum as in rice. A search with 12 NBS domains from published rice, maize, wheat and <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene sequences revealed 211 NBS-LRR coding genes in sorghum, 410 in rice and 149 in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Meyers, B. C. et al. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis . Plant Cell 15, 809â834 (2003)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1592">21</a></sup>. Sorghum NBS-LRR genes mostly encode the CC type of N-terminal domains. Only two sorghum genes (Sb02g005860 and Sb02g036630) contain the TIR domain, and neither contains an NBS domain. NBS-LRR genes are most abundant on sorghum chromosome 5 (62), and its rice homologue (chromosome 11, 106). Enrichment of NBS-LRR genes in these corresponding genomic regions suggests conservation of R gene location, in contrast to a proposal that R gene movement may be advantageous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Leister, D. Tandem and segmental gene duplication and recombination in the evolution of plant disease resistance genes. Trends Genet. 20, 116â122 (2004)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1596">22</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Evolution of distinctive pathways and processes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Evolution of distinctive pathways and processes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>The evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis in the <i>Sorghum</i> lineage involved redirection of C<sub>3</sub> progenitor genes as well as recruitment and functional divergence of both ancient and recent gene duplicates. The sole sorghum C<sub>4</sub> pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase (<i>ppdk</i>) and the phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase kinase (<i>ppck</i>) gene and its two isoforms (produced by the whole genome duplication) have only single orthologues in rice. Additional duplicates formed in maize after the sorghumâmaize split (<i>Zmppck</i>2 and <i>Zmppck</i>3). The C<sub>4</sub> NADP-dependent malic enzyme (<i>me</i>) gene has an adjacent isoform but each corresponds to a different maize homologue, suggesting tandem duplication before the sorghumâmaize split. The C<sub>4</sub> malate dehydrogenase (<i>mdh</i>) gene and its isoform are also adjacent, but share 97% amino acid similarity and correspond to the single known maize <i>Mdh</i> gene, suggesting tandem duplication in sorghum after its split with maize. The rice <i>Me</i> and <i>Mdh</i> genes are single copy, suggesting duplication and recruitment to the C<sub>4</sub> pathway after the PanicoideaeâOryzoideae divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 9</a>).</p><p>The sorghum sequence reinforces inferences previously based only on rice, about how different grass and dicotyledon gene inventories relate to their respective types of cell walls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Carpita, N. C. &amp; Gibeaut, D. M. Structural models of primary cell walls in flowering plantsâconsistency of molecular structure with the physical properties of the walls during growth. Plant J. 3, 1â30 (1993)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1659">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="McCann, M. C. &amp; Roberts, K. in The Cytoskeletal Basis of Plant Growth and Form (ed. Lloyd, C. W.) 109â129 (Academic Press, 1991)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1662">24</a></sup>. In grasses, cellulose microfibrils coated with mixed-linkage (1â3),(1â4)-Î²-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-glucans are interlaced with glucuronoarabinoxylans and an extensive complex of phenylpropanoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Carpita, N. C. Structure and biogenesis of the cell walls of grasses. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 47, 445â476 (1996)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1669">25</a></sup>. The sorghum sequence largely corroborates differences between dicotyledons and rice in the distribution of cell wall biogenesis genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 10</a>). For example, the CesA/Csl superfamily and callose synthases have either diverged to form new subgroups or functionally non-essential subgroups were selectively lost, such as <i>CslB</i> and <i>CslG</i> lost from the grasses, and <i>CslF</i> and <i>CslH</i> lost from species with dicotyledon-like cell walls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Hazen, S. P. et al. Quantitative trait loci and comparative genomics of cereal cell wall composition. Plant Physiol. 132, 263â271 (2003)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1689">26</a></sup>. The previously rice-unique <i>CslF</i> and <i>CslH</i> genes are present in sorghum. <i>Arabidopsis</i> contains a single group F GT31 gene, whereas sorghum and rice contain six and ten, respectively.</p><p>The characteristic adaptation of sorghum to drought may be partly related to expansion of one miRNA and several gene families. Rice miRNA 169g, upregulated during drought stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhao, B. T. et al. Identification of drought-induced microRNAs in rice. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 354, 585â590 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1706">27</a></sup>, has five sorghum homologues (sbi-MIR169c, sbi-MIR169d, sbi-MIR169.p2, sbi-MIR169.p6 and sbi-MIR169.p7). The computationally predicted target of the sbi-MIR169 subfamily comprises members of the plant nuclear factor Y (NF-Y) B transcription factor family, linked to improved performance under drought by <i>Arabidopsis</i> and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Nelson, D. E. et al. Plant nuclear factor Y (NF-Y) B subunits confer drought tolerance and lead to improved corn yields on water-limited acres Proc . Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 16450â16455 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1713">28</a></sup>. Cytochrome P450 domain-containing genes, often involved in scavenging toxins such as those accumulated in response to stress, are abundant in sorghum with 326 versus 228 in rice. Expansins, enzymes that break hydrogen bonds and are responsible for a variety of growth responses that could be linked to the durability of sorghum, occur in 82 copies in sorghum versus 58 in rice and 40 each in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and poplar.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Duplication and diversification of cereal genomes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Duplication and diversification of cereal genomes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Whole-genome duplication in a common ancestor of cereals is reflected in sorghum and rice gene âquartetsâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). A total of 19,929 (57.8%) sorghum gene models were in blocks collinear with rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 6</a>). After the shared whole-genome duplication, only one copy was retained for 13,667 (68.6%) collinear genes with 13,526 (99%) being orthologous in riceâsorghum, indicating that most gene losses predate taxon divergence. Both sorghum and rice retained both copies of 4,912 (14.2%) genes, whereas sorghum lost one copy of 1,070 (3.1%) and rice lost one copy of 634 (1.8%). These patterns are likely to be predictive of other grass genomes, as the major grass lineages diverged from a common ancestor at about the same time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903â9908 (2004)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1734">29</a></sup> (see also <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="
                        Alignment of sorghum, rice and maize.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: 
                        <b>Alignment of sorghum, rice and maize.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="319"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Dot plots show intergenomic (gold) and intragenomic (black) alignments. One sorghumârice quartet showing both orthologous and paralogous (duplicated) regions is magnified. Infrequent gene loss (red; see legend) after sorghumârice divergence causes âspecial casesâ in which there are paralogues but no orthologues. Each sorghum region corresponds to two duplicated maize regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wei, F. et al. Physical and genetic structure of the maize genome reflects its complex evolutionary history. PLoS Genet. 3, e123 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1757">39</a></sup>, with maize gene loss suggested where sorghum loci only match one of the two. Because maize BACs are mostly unfinished, sorghum loci are aligned to the centres. Note the different scale necessary for maize physical distance. Larger dot plots are in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 6</a>.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM285">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although most post-duplication gene loss happened in a common cereal ancestor, some lineage-specific patterns occur. A total of 2 and 10 protein functional (Pfam) domains showed enrichment for duplicates and singletons (respectively) in sorghum but not rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 6.1</a>). Because the sorghumârice divergence is thought to have happened 20âMyr or more after genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903â9908 (2004)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1784">29</a></sup>, this suggests that even long-term gene loss differentially affects gene functional groups.</p><p>One genomic region has been subject to a high level of concerted evolution. It was previously suggested that rice chromosomes 11 and 12 share a <span class="stix">â¼</span>5â7-Myr-old segmental duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Wang, X. et al. Duplication and DNA segmental loss in rice genome and their implications for diploidization. New Phytol. 165, 937â946 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1791">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yu, J. et al. The genomes of Oryza sativa: A history of duplications. PLoS Biol. 3, 266â281 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1794">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="The Rice Chromosomes 11 and 12 Sequencing Consortia The sequence of rice chromosomes 11 and 12, rich in disease resistance genes and recent gene duplications. BMC Biol. 3, 20 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1797">32</a></sup>. We found a duplicated segment in the corresponding regions of sorghum chromosomes 5 and 8 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>). Sorghumâsorghum and riceârice paralogues from this region show rates of synonymous DNA substitution (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) of 0.44 and 0.22, respectively, consistent with only 34 and 17âMyr of divergence. However, the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value of sorghumârice orthologues is 0.63, similar to the respective genome-wide averages (0.81, 0.87). We hypothesize that the apparent segmental duplication actually resulted from the pan-cereal whole-genome duplication and became differentiated from the remainder of the chromosome(s) owing to concerted evolution acting independently in sorghum, rice and perhaps other cereals. Gene conversion and illegitimate recombination are more frequent in the rice 11â12 region than elsewhere in the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang, X. et al. Extensive concerted evolution of rice paralogs and the road to regaining independence. Genetics 177, 1753â1763 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1813">33</a></sup>. Physical and genetic maps suggest shared terminal segments of the corresponding chromosomes in wheat (4, 5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Singh, N. K. et al. Single-copy genes define a conserved order between rice and wheat for understanding differences caused by duplication, deletion, and transposition of genes. Funct. Integr. Genomics 7, 17â35 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1817">34</a></sup>, foxtail millet (VII, VIII) and pearl millet (linkage groups 1, 4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Devos, K. M., Pittaway, T. S., Reynolds, A. &amp; Gale, M. D. Comparative mapping reveals a complex relationship between the pearl millet genome and those of foxtail millet and rice TAG . Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 190â198 (2000)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1821">35</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="
                        Independent illegitimate recombination in corresponding regions of sorghum and rice.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: 
                        <b>Independent illegitimate recombination in corresponding regions of sorghum and rice.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature07723/MediaObjects/41586_2009_Article_BFnature07723_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="600" height="688"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Four homologous rice and sorghum chromosomes (11 and 12 in rice; 5 and 8 in sorghum) are shown, with gene densities plotted. âLâ and âSâ show long and short arms, respectively. Lines show <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> between homologous gene pairs, and colours are used to show different dates of conversion events.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM286">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature07723/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Synthesis and implications"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Synthesis and implications</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Comparison of the sorghum, rice and other genomes clarifies the grass gene set. Pairs of orthologous sorghum and rice genes combined with recent paralogous duplications define 19,542 conserved grass gene families, each representing one gene in the sorghumârice common ancestor. Our sorghum gene count is similar to that in a manually curated rice annotation (RAP2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tanaka, T. et al. The rice annotation project database (RAP-DB): 2008 update. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D1028âD1033 (2008)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1867">36</a></sup>, but this similarity masks some differences. About 2,054 syntenic orthologues shared by our sorghum annotation and the TIGR5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ouyang, S. et al. The TIGR rice genome annotation resource: Improvements and new features. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, D883âD887 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1870">37</a>) rice annotation are absent from RAP2. Conversely, <span class="stix">â¼</span>12,000 TIGR5 annotations may be transposable elements or pseudogenes, comprising large families of hypothetical genes in both sorghum and rice RAP2, often with short exons, few introns and limited EST support. Phylogenetically incongruent cases of apparent gene loss (for example, genes shared by <i>Arabidopsis</i> and sorghum but not rice: <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>) may also suggest sequence gaps or misannotations.</p><p>Grass genome architecture may reflect euchromatin-specific effects of recombination and selection, superimposed on non-adaptive processes of mutation and genetic drift that apply to all genomic regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The origins of genome complexity. Science 302, 1401â1404 (2003)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1883">38</a></sup>. Patterns of gene and repetitive DNA organization remain correlated in homologous chromosomes duplicated 70âMyr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), despite extensive turnover of specific repetitive elements. Synteny is highest and retroelement abundance lowest in distal chromosomal regions. More rapid retroelement removal from gene-rich euchromatin that frequently recombines than from heterochromatin that rarely recombines supports the hypothesis that recombination may preserve gene structure, order and/or spacing by exposing new insertions to selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206â13211 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1890">9</a></sup>. Less euchromatinâheterochromatin polarization in maize, where retrotransposon persistence in euchromatin seems more frequent, may reflect variation in grass genome architecture or perhaps a lingering consequence of more recent genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wei, F. et al. Physical and genetic structure of the maize genome reflects its complex evolutionary history. PLoS Genet. 3, e123 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1894">39</a></sup>.</p><p>Identification of conserved DNA sequences may help us to understand essential genes and binding sites that define grasses. Progress in sequencing <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Huo, N. et al. The nuclear genome of Brachypodium distachyon: Analysis of BAC end sequences. Funct. Integr. Genomics 8, 135â147 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1903">40</a></sup> sets the stage for panicoidâoryzoidâpooid phylogenetic triangulation of genomic changes, as well as association of some such changes with phenotypes ranging from molecular (gene expression patterns) to morphological. The divergence between sorghum, rice and <i>Brachypodium</i> is sufficient to randomize nonfunctional sequence, facilitating conserved noncoding sequence (CNS) discovery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Margulies, E. H. et al. An initial strategy for the systematic identification of functional elements in the human genome by low-redundancy comparative sequencing. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 4795â4800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1910">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Eddy, S. R. A model of the statistical power of comparative genome sequence analysis. PLoS Biol. 3, 95â102 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1913">42</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). More distant comparisons to the dicotyledon <i>Arabidopsis</i> show exon conservation but no CNS (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Chloridoid and arundinoid genome sequences are needed to sample the remaining grass lineages, and an outgroup such as <i>Ananas</i> (pineapple) or <i>Musa</i> (banana) would further aid in identifying genes and sequences that define grasses.</p><p>The fact that the sorghum genome has not re-duplicated in <span class="stix">â¼</span>70âMyr<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903â9908 (2004)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1936">29</a></sup> makes it a valuable outgroup for deducing fates of gene pairs and CNS in grasses that have reduplicated. Single sorghum regions correspond to two regions resulting from maize-specific genome doubling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wei, F. et al. Physical and genetic structure of the maize genome reflects its complex evolutionary history. PLoS Genet. 3, e123 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1940">39</a></sup>âgene fractionation is evident (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>), and subfunctionalization is probable (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Sorghum may prove especially valuable for unravelling genome evolution in the more closely related <i>Saccharum</i>â<i>Miscanthus</i> clade: two genome duplications since its divergence from sorghum 8â9âMyr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jannoo, N. et al. Orthologous comparison in a gene-rich region among grasses reveals stability in the sugarcane polyploid genome. Plant J. 50, 574â585 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1957">43</a></sup> complicate sugar cane genetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Ming, R. et al. Sugarcane improvement through breeding and biotechnology. Plant Breed. Rev. 27, 15â118 (2005)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1961">44</a></sup> yet <i>Saccharum</i> BACs show substantially conserved gene order with sorghum (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 11</a>).</p><p>Conservation of grass gene structure and order facilitates development of DNA markers to support crop improvement. We identified <span class="stix">â¼</span>71,000 simple-sequence repeats (SSRs) in sorghum (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary List 1</a>); among a sampling of 212, only 9 (4.2%) map to paralogues of their source locus. Conserved-intron scanning primers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary List 2</a>) for 6,760 genes provide DNA markers useful across many monocotyledons, particularly valuable for âorphan cerealsâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Lohithaswa, H. C. et al. Leveraging the rice genome sequence for comparative genomics in monocots. Theor. Appl. Genet. 115, 237â243 (2007)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1981">45</a></sup>.</p><p>As the first sequenced plant genome of African origin, sorghum adds new dimensions to ethnobotanical research. Of particular interest will be the identification of alleles selected during the earliest stages of sorghum cultivation, which are valuable towards testing the hypothesis that convergent mutations in corresponding genes contributed to independent domestications of divergent cereals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Convergent domestication of cereal crops by independent mutations at corresponding genetic loci. Science 269, 1714â1718 (1995)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d2886e1988">46</a></sup>. Invigorated sorghum improvement would benefit regions such as the African âSahelâ where drought tolerance makes sorghum a staple for human populations that are increasing by 2.8% per year. Sorghum yield improvement has lagged behind that of other grains, in Africa only gaining a total of 37% (western) to 38% (eastern) from 1961â63 to 2005â07 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 12</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing</h3><p>Approximately 8.5-fold redundant paired-end shotgun sequencing was performed using standard Sanger methodologies from small (<span class="stix">â¼</span>2â3âkb) and medium (5â8 kb) insert plasmid libraries, one fosmid library (<span class="stix">â¼</span>35 kb inserts), and two BAC libraries (insert size 90 and 108âkb). (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 1</a>.)</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Integration of shotgun assembly with genetic and physical maps</h3><p>The largest 201 scaffolds, all exceeding 39âkb, excluding âNâs, and collectively representing 678,902,941âbp (97.3%) of nucleotides, were checked for possible chimaeras suggested by the sorghum genetic map, sorghum physical map, abrupt changes in gene or repeat density, rice gene order, and coverage by BAC or fosmid clones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Repeat analysis</h3><p><i>De novo</i> searches for LTR retrotransposons used LTR_STRUC. <i>De novo</i> detection of CACTA-DNA transposons and MITEs used custom programs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature07723#MOESM287">Supplementary Note 3</a>). Known repeats were identified by RepeatMasker (Open-3-1-8) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) with mips-REdat_6.2_Poaceae, a compilation of grass repeats including sorghum-specific LTR retrotransposons (<a href="http://mips.gsf.de/proj/plant/webapp/recat/">http://mips.gsf.de/proj/plant/webapp/recat/</a>). The insertion age of full-length LTR-retrotransposons was determined from the evolutionary distance between 5â² and 3â² soloLTR derived from a ClustalW alignment of the two soloLTRs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Protein-coding gene annotation</h3><p>Putative protein-coding loci were identified based on BLAST alignments of rice and <i>Arabidopsis</i> peptides and sorghum and maize ESTs. GenomeScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Yeh, R.-F., Lim, L. P. &amp; Burge, C. Computational inference of homologous gene structures in the human genome. Genome Res. 11, 803â816 (2001)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d2886e2062">47</a></sup> was applied using maize-specific parameters. Predicted coding structures were merged with EST data from maize and sorghum using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Full-length messenger RNA sequences greatly improve genome annotation. Genome Biol. 3, research0029.0021â0029.0012 (2002)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d2886e2066">48</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Intergenomic and intragenomic alignments</h3><p>Dot plots used ColinearScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wang, X. Y. et al. Statistical inference of chromosomal homology based on gene colinearity and applications to Arabidopsis and rice. BMC Bioinform. 7, 447 (2006)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d2886e2079">49</a></sup> and multi-alignments used MCScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and colinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486â488 (2008)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d2886e2083">50</a></sup>, applied to RAP2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tanaka, T. et al. The rice annotation project database (RAP-DB): 2008 update. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D1028âD1033 (2008)" href="/articles/nature07723#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d2886e2087">36</a></sup> (mapped representative models, 29,389 loci) and the sbi1.4 annotation set (34,496 loci). Pairwise BLASTP (<i>E</i>â&lt;â1Ã10<sup>-5</sup>, top five hits), both within each genome and between the two genomes, was used to retrieve potential anchors. <i>Zea</i> BAC sequences and FPC contig coordinates were downloaded (<a href="http://www.maizesequence.org">http://www.maizesequence.org</a>, release 7 January 2008). <i>Zea</i> BACs were searched for potential orthologues of <i>Sorghum</i> coding sequences using translated BLAT with a minimum score of 100.</p></div></div></section>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title"><i>Sorghum</i> genome: drought tolerance in the genes</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> genome sequence is published this week. Sorghum is a cereal grown widely as food, animal feed, fibre and fuel. Tolerant to hot, dry conditions, it is a staple for large populations in the West African Sahel region. Comparisons of the genome with those of maize and rice shed light on the evolution of grasses and of C4 photosynthesis, which is particularly efficient at assimilating carbon at high temperatures. In addition, protein coding genes and miRNAs that could contribute to sorghum's drought tolerance may also be found. Sorghum yield improvement has lagged behind that of other crops and the availability of the genome sequence could provide a vital boost to work on its improvement.</p></div></section></div></aside>
